<p><b>Muhammadu Buhari</b> <span style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Federal_Republic" title="Order of the Federal Republic">GCFR</a></span> (born 17 December 1942) is the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Nigeria" title="President of Nigeria">President</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, in office since 2015. He is a retired <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_General" title="Major General">Major General</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Army" title="Nigerian Army">Nigerian Army</a> and previously served as the nation's Head of State from 31 December 1983 to 27 August 1985, after taking power in a <a href="/wiki/1983_Nigerian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1983 Nigerian coup d'état">military <i>coup d'état</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buhari-Idiagbon_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buhari-Idiagbon-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siollun_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siollun-7">[7]</a></sup> The term <a href="/wiki/Buharism" title="Buharism">Buharism</a> is ascribed to the Buhari military government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanusi_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanusi-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nura_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nura-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>He unsuccessfully ran for the office of President in the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_presidential_election,_2003" title="Nigerian presidential election, 2003">2003</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_general_election,_2007" title="Nigerian general election, 2007">2007</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_presidential_election,_2011" title="Nigerian presidential election, 2011">2011</a> general elections. In December 2014, he emerged as the presidential candidate of the <a href="/wiki/All_Progressives_Congress" title="All Progressives Congress">All Progressives Congress</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_general_election,_2015" title="Nigerian general election, 2015">March 2015 general elections</a>. Buhari won the election, defeating the incumbent President <a href="/wiki/Goodluck_Jonathan" title="Goodluck Jonathan">Goodluck Jonathan</a>. This marked the first time in the history of Nigeria that an incumbent president lost to an opposition candidate in a general election. He was <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Muhammadu_Buhari" title="Inauguration of Muhammadu Buhari">sworn in</a> on 29 May 2015.</p>
<p>Buhari has stated that he takes responsibility for anything over which he presided during his military rule, and that he cannot change the past. He has described himself as a "converted democrat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Muhammadu Buhari was born on 17 December 1942, in <a href="/wiki/Daura" title="Daura">Daura</a>, Katsina State, to his father Adamu and mother Zulaihat. He is the twenty-third child of his father. Buhari was raised by his mother, after his father died when he was about four years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>He attended primary school in Daura and Mai'adua before proceeding to Katsina Model School in 1953, and to Katsina Provincial Secondary School (now Government College Katsina) from 1956 to 1961.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_military_career">Early military career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early military career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Buhari joined the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Army" title="Nigerian Army">Nigerian Army</a> by enrolling in the Nigerian Military Training College (NMTC) in 1961. In February 1964, the college was upgraded to an officer commissioning unit of the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Army" title="Nigerian Army">Nigerian Army</a> and renamed the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Defence_Academy" title="Nigerian Defence Academy">Nigerian Defence Academy</a> (NDA) (prior to 1964, the Nigerian government sent cadets who had completed their NMTC preliminary training to mostly <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> military academies <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ogbebor_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ogbebor-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agbese_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agbese-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luckham_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luckham-14">[14]</a></sup> for officer cadet training). From 1962 to 1963, Buhari underwent officer cadet training at <a href="/wiki/Mons_Officer_Cadet_School" title="Mons Officer Cadet School">Mons Officer Cadet School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Aldershot" title="Aldershot">Aldershot</a> in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.</p>
<p>In January 1963, Buhari was commissioned a second lieutenant and appointed Platoon Commander of the Second Infantry Battalion in <a href="/wiki/Abeokuta" title="Abeokuta">Abeokuta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>. From November 1963 to January 1964, Buhari attended the Platoon Commanders' Course at the Nigerian Military Training College, <a href="/wiki/Kaduna" title="Kaduna">Kaduna</a>. In 1964, he facilitated his military training by attending the Mechanical Transport Officer's Course at the Army Mechanical Transport School in Borden, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>.</p>
<p>From 1965 to 1967, Buhari served as Commander of the Second Infantry Battalion and appointed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brigade_Major" title="Brigade Major">Brigade Major</a>, Second Sector, First Infantry Division, April 1967 to July 1967.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_counter-coup_of_28_July_1966">Northern counter-coup of 28 July 1966</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Northern counter-coup of 28 July 1966">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In July 1966 <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant" title="Lieutenant">Lieutenant</a> Muhammadu Buhari was one of the participants in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nigerian_Counter-Coup_of_1966" title="Nigerian Counter-Coup of 1966">coup</a>, led by Lt-Col <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Murtala_Muhammed" title="Murtala Muhammed">Murtala Muhammed</a>, that overthrew and assassinated Nigeria's first self-appointed military Head of State General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aguiyi_Ironsi" title="Aguiyi Ironsi">Aguiyi Ironsi</a>, who had assumed leadership of the Nigerian government after a <a href="/wiki/1966_Nigerian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1966 Nigerian coup d'état">failed coup attempt on 15 January 1966</a>, which overthrew the elected parliamentary government of Nigeria (also known as first republic). Other participants in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nigerian_Counter-Coup_of_1966" title="Nigerian Counter-Coup of 1966">coup on 28 July 1966</a> included 2nd Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Sani_Abacha" title="Sani Abacha">Sani Abacha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant" title="Lieutenant">Lieutenant</a> <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Babangida" title="Ibrahim Babangida">Ibrahim Babangida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Major" title="Major">Major</a> <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Danjuma" title="Theophilus Danjuma">Theophilus Danjuma</a>, Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Bako" title="Ibrahim Bako">Ibrahim Bako</a> among others. The coup was a reaction to the <a href="/wiki/1966_Nigerian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1966 Nigerian coup d'état">January coup</a> where a group of mostly Igbo officers led by Major <a href="/wiki/Chukwuma_Kaduna_Nzeogwu" title="Chukwuma Kaduna Nzeogwu">Chukwuma Kaduna Nzeogwu</a> overthrew the democratically elected government of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Abubakar_Tafawa_Balewa" title="Abubakar Tafawa Balewa">Abubakar Tafawa Balewa</a>. Many Northern soldiers were aggrieved by the murder of senior politicians, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Abubakar_Tafawa_Balewa" title="Abubakar Tafawa Balewa">Abubakar Tafawa Balewa</a>, northern regional premier, <a href="/wiki/Ahmadu_Bello" title="Ahmadu Bello">Ahmadu Bello</a>, and four senior officers from northern Nigeria: Brigadier Zakariya Maimalari, Colonel Kur Mohammed, Lt-Cols Abogo Largema and James Pam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siollun-2_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siollun-2-15">[15]</a></sup> The counter-coup was very bloody leading to the murder of mostly Igbo officers. Among the casualties were the first military head of state General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aguiyi_Ironsi" title="Aguiyi Ironsi">Aguiyi Ironsi</a> and Lt Colonel <a href="/wiki/Adekunle_Fajuyi" title="Adekunle Fajuyi">Adekunle Fajuyi</a>, the military governor of the Western Region.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war">Civil war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Civil war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Buhari was assigned to the <a href="/wiki/1st_Division_(Nigeria)" title="1st Division (Nigeria)">1st Division</a> under the command of Lt. Col <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Shuwa" title="Mohammed Shuwa">Mohammed Shuwa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMomoh200065_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMomoh200065-16">[16]</a></sup> the division had temporarily moved from Kaduna to <a href="/wiki/Makurdi" title="Makurdi">Makurdi</a> at the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Civil_War" title="Nigerian Civil War">Nigerian Civil War</a>. The 1st division was divided into sectors and then battalions <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMomoh2000343_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMomoh2000343-17">[17]</a></sup> with Shuwa assisted by sector commanders Martin Adamu and Sule Apollo who was later replaced by <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Danjuma" title="Theophilus Danjuma">Theophilus Danjuma</a>. Buhari's initial assignment was as <a href="/wiki/Adjutant" title="Adjutant">Adjutant</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Company_Commander" title="Company Commander">Company Commander</a> 2 battalion unit, Second Sector Infantry of the 1st Division. The 2 battalion was one of the units that participated in the first actions of the war, they started from Gakem near <a href="/wiki/Afikpo" title="Afikpo">Afikpo</a> and moved towards <a href="/wiki/Ogoja" title="Ogoja">Ogoja</a> with support from <a href="/wiki/Gado_Nasko" title="Gado Nasko">Gado Nasko</a>'s artillery squad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMomoh200069_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMomoh200069-18">[18]</a></sup> They reached and captured Ogoja within a week with the intention of advancing through the flanks to <a href="/wiki/Enugu" title="Enugu">Enugu</a>, the rebel capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMomoh2000339_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMomoh2000339-19">[19]</a></sup> Buhari was briefly the 2 battalion's Commander and led the battalion to Afikpo to link with the 3rd Marine Commando and advance towards Enugu through Nkalagu and <a href="/wiki/Abakaliki" title="Abakaliki">Abakaliki</a>. However, before the move to Enugu, he was posted to Nsukka as Brigade Major of the 3rd Infantry Brigade under Joshua Gin who would later become battle fatigued and replaced by Isa Bukar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMomoh2000340_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMomoh2000340-20">[20]</a></sup> Buhari stayed with the infantry for a few months has the Nigerian army began to adjust tactics learnt from early battle experiences. Instead of swift advances, the new tactics involved securing and holding on to the lines of communications and using captured towns as training ground to train new recruits brought in from the Army depots in Abeokuta and Zaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMomoh2000340_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMomoh2000340-20">[20]</a></sup> In 1968, he was posted to the 4 Sector also called the Awka sector which was charged to take over the capture of Onitsha from Division 2. The sector's operations was within the <a href="/wiki/Awka" title="Awka">Awka</a>-<a href="/wiki/Abagana" title="Abagana">Abagana</a>-Onitsha region which was important to Biafran forces because it was a major source of food supply. It was in the sector that Buhari's group suffered a lot of casualties trying to protect food supplies route of the rebels along <a href="/wiki/Oji_River" title="Oji River">Oji River</a> and Abagana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMomoh200078_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMomoh200078-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="After_the_war">After the war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: After the war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From 1970 to 1971, Buhari was Brigade Major/Commandant, Thirty-first Infantry Brigade. He then served as the Assistant Adjutant-General, First Infantry Division Headquarters, from 1971 to 1972. He also attended the <a href="/wiki/Defence_Services_Staff_College" title="Defence Services Staff College">Defence Services Staff College</a>, Wellington, India, in 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quartz_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quartz-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1974 to 1975 Buhari was Acting Director of Transport and Supply at the Nigerian Army Corps of Supply and Transport Headquarters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buhari.27s_participation_in_July_1975_coup">Buhari's participation in July 1975 coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Buhari's participation in July 1975 coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Colonel" title="Lieutenant Colonel">Lieutenant Colonel</a> Buhari was among a group of officers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-Farnsworth_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-Farnsworth-24">[24]</a></sup> (led<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waado-Omoigui_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waado-Omoigui-25">[25]</a></sup> by Colonels <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Taiwo" title="Ibrahim Taiwo">Ibrahim Taiwo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Garba" title="Joseph Garba">Joseph Garba</a>, Abdulahi Mohammed, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ochefu" title="Anthony Ochefu">Anthony Ochefu</a>, Lieutenant Colonels <a href="/wiki/Shehu_Musa_Yar%27Adua" title="Shehu Musa Yar'Adua">Shehu Musa Yar'Adua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Babangida" title="Ibrahim Babangida">Ibrahim Babangida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Aduloju" title="Alfred Aduloju">Alfred Aduloju</a>) who overthrew the Head of State, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General" title="General">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Yakubu_Gowon" title="Yakubu Gowon">Yakubu Gowon</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor_of_North_Eastern_State">Governor of North Eastern State</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Governor of North Eastern State">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From 1 August 1975 to 3 February 1976, General <a href="/wiki/Murtala_Mohammed" title="Murtala Mohammed">Murtala Mohammed</a>, appointed Buhari as Governor of the <a href="/wiki/North-Eastern_State" title="North-Eastern State">North-Eastern State</a>, to oversee social, economic and political improvements in the state. Buhari also briefly served as Governor of Borno state from 3 February 1976 to 15 March 1976.</p>
<p>On 3 February 1976, the North Eastern state was divided by the Military Government into Bauchi, Borno and Gongola states. In August 1991, Yobe state was created from Borno state, while Gongola state was split into two states, Taraba and Adamawa. In October 1996, Gombe State was created from Bauchi State.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_Commissioner_for_Petroleum_and_Natural_Resources">Federal Commissioner for Petroleum and Natural Resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Federal Commissioner for Petroleum and Natural Resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In March 1976, the Head of State, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General" title="General">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Olusegun_Obasanjo" title="Olusegun Obasanjo">Olusegun Obasanjo</a>, appointed Buhari as the Federal Commissioner (position now called Minister) for Petroleum and Natural Resources. When the Nigerian National Petroleum Corporation was created in 1977,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Buhari was also appointed as its Chairman, a position he held until 1978. During his tenure as Commissioner, 2.8 billion Naira allegedly went missing from the accounts of the NNPC in Midlands Bank in the United Kingdom. Former President Ibrahim Babangida allegedly accused Buhari of being responsible for this fraud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, in the conclusion of the Crude Oil Sales Tribunal of Inquiry headed by Justice <a href="/wiki/Ayo_Gabriel_Irikefe" title="Ayo Gabriel Irikefe">Ayo Irikefe</a> to investigate allegations of N2.8 billion misappropriation from the NNPC account. The tribunal found no truth in the allegations even though it noticed some lapses in the NNPC accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>During Buhari's tenure as the Federal Commissioner for Petroleum and Natural Resources, the government invested in pipelines and petroleum storage infrastructures. The government built about 21 petroleum storage depots all over the country from Lagos to Maidugiuri and from Calabar to Gusau; the administration constructed a pipeline network that connected Bonny terminal and the Port Harcourt refinery to the depots. Also, the administration signed the contract for the construction of a refinery in Kaduna and an oil pipeline that will connect the Escravos oil terminal to Warri Refinery and the proposed Kaduna refinery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Back_in_military_service">Back in military service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Back in military service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From 1978 to 1979, he was Military Secretary at the Army Headquarters and was a member of the Supreme Military Council from 1978 to 1979. From 1979 to 1980, at the rank of colonel, Buhari (class of 1980) attended the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Army_War_College" title="US Army War College">US Army War College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Carlisle,_Pennsylvania" title="Carlisle, Pennsylvania">Carlisle, Pennsylvania</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, and gained a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masters_Degree" title="Masters Degree">Masters Degree</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strategic_Studies" title="Strategic Studies">Strategic Studies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Upon completion of the on-campus full-time resident program lasting ten months and the two-year-long, distance learning program, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_War_College" title="United States Army War College">United States Army War College</a> (USAWC) college awards its graduate officers a master's degree in Strategic Studies.</p>
<p>Other roles include:</p>
<ul>
<li>General Officer Commanding, 4th Infantry Division, August 1980 – January 1981<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>General Officer Commanding, 2nd Mechanised Infantry Division, January 1981 – October 1981<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>General Officer Commanding, 3rd Armed Division Nigerian Army, October 1981 – December 1983</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chadian_military_affair">Chadian military affair</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Chadian military affair">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1983, when Chadian forces invaded Nigeria in the Borno State, Buhari used the forces under his command to chase them out of the country, crossing into Chadian territory in spite of an order given by President Shagari to withdraw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ayoade_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ayoade-36">[36]</a></sup> This 1983 Chadian military affair led to more than 100 victims and "prisoners of war".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ayoade_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ayoade-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="December_1983_military_coup">December 1983 military coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: December 1983 military coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1983_Nigerian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1983 Nigerian coup d'état">1983 Nigerian coup d'état</a></div>
<p>Major-General Buhari was one of the leaders of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nigerian_military_coup_of_December_31,_1983" title="Nigerian military coup of December 31, 1983">military coup of December 1983</a> that overthrew the democratically elected government of President Shehu Shagari. At the time of the coup plot, Buhari was the General Officer Commanding (GOC), Third Armored Division of <a href="/wiki/Jos" title="Jos">Jos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> With the successful execution of the coup by General Buhari, <a href="/wiki/Tunde_Idiagbon" title="Tunde Idiagbon">Tunde Idiagbon</a> was appointed Chief of General Staff (the de facto No. 2 in the administration). The coup ended Nigeria's short-lived Second Republic, a period of multi-party democracy started in 1979. According to <i>The New York Times</i>, the officers who took power argued that "a flawed democracy was worse than no democracy at all". Buhari justified the military's seizure of power by castigating the civilian government as hopelessly corrupt and promptly suspended Nigeria's 1979 Constitution. Another rationale for the coup was to correct economic decline in Nigeria. Sani Abacha in the military's first broadcast after the coup linked ' an inept and corrupt leadership'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraf1988149_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraf1988149-38">[38]</a></sup> with general economic decline. In Buhari's New Year day speech, he too mentioned the corrupt class of the second republic but also as the cause of a general decline in morality in the society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraf1988149_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraf1988149-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Head_of_state_.281983.E2.80.9385.29">Head of state (1983–85)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Head of state (1983–85)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The structure of the new military leadership which was also the fifth in Nigeria since independence resembled the last military regime, the Obasanjo/Yaradua administration. The new regime established a Supreme Military Council, a Federal Executive Council and a Council of States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraf1988150_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraf1988150-39">[39]</a></sup> The number of ministries was trimmed to 18 while the administration carried out a retrenchment exercise among the senior ranks of the civil service and police. It retired 17 permanent secretaries and some senior police and naval officers. In addition, the new military administration promulgated new laws to achieve its aim. These laws included the Robbery and Firearms (Special Provisions) Decree for the prosecution of armed robbery cases, the State Security (Detention of Person) Decree which gave powers to the military to detain individuals suspected of jeopardizing state security or causing economic adversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraf1988153_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraf1988153-40">[40]</a></sup> Other decrees included the Civil Service Commission and Public Offenders Decree which constituted the legal and administrative basis to conduct a purge in the civil service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraf1988153_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraf1988153-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Policies_and_initiatives">Policies and initiatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Policies and initiatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_policy">Economic policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Economic policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In order to reform the economy, as Head of State, Buhari started to rebuild the nation's social-political and economic systems, along the realities of Nigeria's austere economic conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-upa_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-upa-41">[41]</a></sup> The rebuilding included removing or cutting back the excesses in national expenditure, obliterating or removing completely, corruption from the nation's social ethics, shifting from mainly public sector employment to self-employment. Buhari also encouraged import substitution industrialisation based to a great extent on the use of local materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-upa_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-upa-41">[41]</a></sup> However, tightening of imports led to reduction in raw materials for industries causing many industries to operate below capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraf1988162_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraf1988162-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, Buhari's bid to re-balance public finances by curbing imports led to many job losses and the closure of businesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Buhari broke ties with the International Monetary Fund, when the fund asked the government to devalue the naira by 60%. However, the reforms that Buhari instigated on his own were as or more rigorous as those required by the IMF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 7 May 1984, Buhari announced the country's 1984 National Budget. The budget came with a series of complementary measures:</p>
<ul>
<li>A temporary ban on recruiting federal public sector workers</li>
<li>Raising of Interest rates</li>
<li>Halting Capital Projects</li>
<li>Prohibition of borrowing by State governments</li>
<li>15 percent cut from Shagari's 1983 Budget</li>
<li>Realignment of import duties</li>
<li>Reducing the balance of payment deficit by cutting imports</li>
<li>It also gave priority to the importation of raw materials and spare parts that were needed for agriculture and industry.</li>
</ul>
<p>Other economic measures by Buhari took the form of counter trade, currency change, price reduction of goods and services.</p>
<p>Buhari's economic policies did not earn him the legitimacy of the masses due to the rise in inflation and the use of military might to continue to push many policies blamed for the rise in food prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraf1988164_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraf1988164-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Buhari's military government continued largely with the foreign policy it inherited from <a href="/wiki/Shehu_Shagari" title="Shehu Shagari">Shehu Shagari</a>. In January 1984, in his new year broadcast speech, Buhari stated that he would maintain and enhance diplomatic relations with all countries and international organisations such as the OAU, UN, OPEC, ECOWAS and the Commonwealth of Nations. He also stated that he would honour all treaty obligations entered into by previous governments, which he did.</p>
<p>Buhari's foreign policy also focused on Africa, mostly Nigeria's neighbours due to financial commitments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="War_on_corruption">War on corruption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: War on corruption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Buhari mounted an offensive against entrenched interests. In 20 months as Head of State, about 500 politicians, officials and businessmen were jailed for corruption during his stewardship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Detainees were released after releasing sums to the government and agreeing to meet certain conditions.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dikko_Affair" title="Dikko Affair">The Umaru Dikko Affair</a> was another defining moment in Buhari's military government. <a href="/wiki/Umaru_Dikko" title="Umaru Dikko">Umaru Dikko</a>, a former Minister of Transportation under the previous civilian administration of President Shagari who fled the country shortly after the coup, was accused of embezzling $1 billion in oil profits. With the help of an alleged former Mossad agent, the NSO traced him to London, where operatives from Nigeria and Israel drugged and kidnapped him. They placed him in a plastic bag, which was subsequently hidden inside a crate labelled as "Diplomatic Baggage". The purpose of this secret operation was to ship Dikko off to Nigeria on an empty Nigerian Airways Boeing 707, to stand trial for embezzlement. The plot was foiled by British airport officers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Buhari's administration enacted three decrees to investigate corruption and control foreign exchange. The Banking (Freezing of Accounts) Decree of 1984, allotted to the Federal Military Government the power to freeze bank accounts of persons suspected to have committed fraud. The Recovery of Public Property (Special Military Tribunals) Decree permitted the government to investigate the assets of public officials linked with corruption and constitute a military tribunal to try such persons. The Exchange Control (Anti-Sabotage) Decree stated penalties for violators of foreign exchange laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraf1988154_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraf1988154-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="War_Against_Indiscipline">War Against Indiscipline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: War Against Indiscipline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>One of the most enduring legacies of the Buhari government has been the War Against Indiscipline (WAI). Launched on 20 March 1984, the policy tried to address the perceived lack of public morality and civic responsibility of Nigerian society. Unruly Nigerians were ordered to form neat queues at bus stops, under the eyes of whip-wielding soldiers. Civil servants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> who failed to show up on time at work were humiliated and forced to do "frog jumps". Minor offences carried long sentences. Any student over the age of 17 caught cheating on an exam would get 21 years in prison. Counterfeiting and arson could lead to the death penalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="53_suitcases_saga">53 suitcases saga</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 53 suitcases saga">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Buhari's administration was embroiled in a scandal concerning the fate of 53 suitcases with unknown contents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The suitcases were being transported by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Emir_of_Gwandu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Emir of Gwandu (page does not exist)">Emir of Gwandu</a>, whose son was Buhari's aide-de-camp, and were cleared through customs on 10 June 1984 without inspection during his return flight from Saudi Arabia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Human rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to Decree Number 2 of 1984, the state security and the chief of staff were given the power to detain, without charges, individuals deemed to be a security risk to the state for up to three months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Strikes and popular demonstrations were banned and Nigeria's security agency, the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Organization" title="National Security Organization">National Security Organization</a> (NSO) was entrusted with unprecedented powers. The NSO played a wide role in the cracking down of public dissent by intimidating, harassing and jailing individuals who broke the interdiction on strikes. By October 1984, about 200,000 civil servants were retrenched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The regime also jailed its critics, as in the case of Nigeria's most popular artist and one time presidential contender, afro-beat singer <a href="/wiki/Fela_Kuti" title="Fela Kuti">Fela Kuti</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amnesty_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty-57">[57]</a></sup> He was arrested on 4 September 1984 at the airport as he was about to embark on an American tour. <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> described the charges brought against him for illegally exporting foreign currency as "spurious". Using the wide powers bestowed upon it by Decree Number 2, the government sentenced Fela to five years in prison. He was released after 18 months,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amnesty_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty-57">[57]</a></sup> when the Buhari government was toppled in a <i>coup d'état</i>.</p>
<p>In 1984, Buhari passed Decree Number 4, the Protection Against False Accusations Decree,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> considered by scholars as the most repressive press law ever enacted in Nigeria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nairaland1_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-nairaland1-59">[59]</a></sup> Section 1 of the law provided that "Any person who publishes in any form, whether written or otherwise, any message, rumour, report or statement [...] which is false in any material particular or which brings or is calculated to bring the Federal Military Government or the Government of a state or public officer to ridicule or disrepute, shall be guilty of an offense under this Decree".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The law further stated that offending journalists and publishers will be tried by an open military tribunal, whose ruling would be final and unappealable in any court and those found guilty would be eligible for a fine not less than 10,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naira" title="Naira">naira</a> and a jail sentence of up to two years. Tunde Thompson and Nduka Irabor of The Guardian were among the journalists who were tried under the decree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nairaland1_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-nairaland1-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Decree 20 on illegal ship bunkering and drug trafficking was another example of Buhari's tough approach to crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Section 3 (2) (K) provided that "any person who, without lawful authority deals in, sells, smokes or inhales the drug known as cocaine or other similar drugs, shall be guilty under section 6 (3) (K) of an offence and liable on conviction to suffer death sentence by firing squad." In the case of Bernard Ogedengebe, the Decree was applied retroactively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tdl_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-tdl-62">[62]</a></sup> He was executed even if at the time of his arrest the crime did not mandate the capital punishment, but had carried a sentence of six months imprisonment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tdl_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-tdl-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>In another prominent case of April 1985, six Nigerians were condemned to death under the same decree: Sidikatu Tairi, Sola Oguntayo, Oladele Omosebi, Lasunkanmi Awolola, Jimi Adebayo and Gladys Iyamah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1985, prompted by economic uncertainties and a rising crime rate, the government of Buhari opened the borders (closed since April 1984) with <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> to speed up the expulsion of 700,000 illegal foreigners and illegal migrant workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Buhari is today known for this crisis; there even is a famine in the east of Niger that have been named "El Buhari".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>His regime drew criticism from many, including Nigeria's first Nobel Prize winner Wole Soyinka, who, in 2007, wrote a piece called "The Crimes of Buhari"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> which outlined many of the abuses conducted under his military rule.</p>
<p>Ahead of the 2015 general election, Buhari responded to his human rights criticism by saying that if elected, he would follow the rule of law, and that there would be access to justice for all Nigerians and respect for fundamental human rights of Nigerians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1985_coup_and_detention">1985 coup and detention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: 1985 coup and detention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In August 1985, Major General Buhari was overthrown in a coup led by General <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Babangida" title="Ibrahim Babangida">Ibrahim Babangida</a> and other members of the ruling Supreme Military Council (SMC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Babangida brought many of Buhari's most vocal critics into his administration, including <a href="/wiki/Fela_Kuti" title="Fela Kuti">Fela Kuti</a>'s brother <a href="/wiki/Olikoye_Ransome-Kuti" title="Olikoye Ransome-Kuti">Olikoye Ransome-Kuti</a>, a doctor who had led a strike against Buhari to protest declining health care services. Buhari was then detained in <a href="/wiki/Benin_City" title="Benin City">Benin City</a> until 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Buhari's admirers believe that he was overthrown by corrupt elements in his government who were afraid of being brought to justice as his policies were beginning to yield tangible dividends in terms of public discipline, curbing corruption, lowering inflation, enhancing workforce and improving productivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siollun_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Siollun-7">[7]</a></sup> Ibrahim Babangida justified his <i><a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a></i> by saying that Buhari failed to deal with the country's economic problems and promised "to rejuvenate the economy ravaged by decades of government mismanagement and corruption".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Buhari spent three years of detention in a small guarded bungalow in Benin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paden_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paden-3">[3]</a></sup> He had access to television that showed two channels and members of his family were allowed to visit him on the authorization of Babangida.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_retirement">In retirement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: In retirement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civilian_life">Civilian life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Civilian life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After his mother's death, he was released in December 1988 and went into farming. While in detention, his farm was managed by his relatives. He divorced his first wife in 1988 and married Aisha Halilu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paden_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Paden-3">[3]</a></sup> In Katsina, he became the pioneer chairman of Katsina Foundation that was founded to encourage social and economic development in Katsina State.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chairman_of_the_Petroleum_Trust_Fund">Chairman of the Petroleum Trust Fund</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Chairman of the Petroleum Trust Fund">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Buhari served as the Chairman of the Petroleum Trust Fund (PTF), a body created by the government of General <a href="/wiki/Sani_Abacha" title="Sani Abacha">Sani Abacha</a>, and funded from the revenue generated by the increase in price of petroleum products, to pursue developmental projects around the country. A 1998 report in <i><a href="/wiki/New_African" title="New African">New African</a></i> praised the PTF under Buhari for its transparency, calling it a rare "success story".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PTF_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-PTF-71">[71]</a></sup> However, the same report also noted that critics had questioned the PTF's allocation of 20% of its resources to the military, which the critics feared would not be accountable for the revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PTF_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-PTF-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_campaigns_and_elections">Presidential campaigns and elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Presidential campaigns and elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2003_presidential_elections">2003 presidential elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: 2003 presidential elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2003, Buhari ran for office in the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_presidential_election,_2003" title="Nigerian presidential election, 2003">presidential election</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> as the candidate of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All_Nigeria_People%27s_Party" title="All Nigeria People's Party">All Nigeria People's Party</a> (ANPP). He was defeated by the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Party_(Nigeria)" title="People's Democratic Party (Nigeria)">People's Democratic Party</a> nominee, President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olus%E1%BA%B9gun_%E1%BB%8Cbasanj%E1%BB%8D" title="Olusẹgun Ọbasanjọ">Olusẹgun Ọbasanjọ</a>, by a margin of more than 11 million votes.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2007_presidential_elections">2007 presidential elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 2007 presidential elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 18 December 2006, Gen. Buhari was nominated as the consensus candidate of the All Nigeria People's Party. His main challenger in the April 2007 polls was the ruling PDP candidate, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umaru_Yar%27Adua" title="Umaru Yar'Adua">Umaru Yar'Adua</a>, who hailed from the same home state of <a href="/wiki/Katsina_State" title="Katsina State">Katsina</a>. In the election, Buhari officially took 18% of the vote against 70% for Yar'Adua, but Buhari rejected these results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huge_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huge-73">[73]</a></sup> After Yar'Adua took office, the ANPP agreed to join his government, but Buhari denounced this agreement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2011_presidential_elections">2011 presidential elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 2011 presidential elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In March 2010, Buhari left the ANPP for the <a href="/wiki/Congress_for_Progressive_Change" title="Congress for Progressive Change">Congress for Progressive Change</a> (CPC), a party that he had helped to found. He said that he had supported foundation of the CPC "as a solution to the debilitating, ethical and ideological conflicts in my former party the ANPP".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mamah20100318_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mamah20100318-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Buhari was the CPC Presidential candidate in 16 April 2011 general election, running against incumbent President <a href="/wiki/Goodluck_Jonathan" title="Goodluck Jonathan">Goodluck Jonathan</a> of the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Party_(Nigeria)" title="People's Democratic Party (Nigeria)">People's Democratic Party</a> (PDP), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mallam_Nuhu_Ribadu" title="Mallam Nuhu Ribadu">Mallam Nuhu Ribadu</a> of <a href="/wiki/Action_Congress_of_Nigeria" title="Action Congress of Nigeria">Action Congress of Nigeria</a> (ACN), and <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Shekarau" title="Ibrahim Shekarau">Ibrahim Shekarau</a> of ANPP. They were the major contenders among 20 contestants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INEC_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-INEC-76">[76]</a></sup> He was campaigning on an anti-corruption platform and pledged to remove immunity protections from government officials. He also gave support to enforcement of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharia_law" title="Sharia law">Sharia law</a> in Nigeria's northern states, which had previously caused him political difficulties among Christian voters in the country's south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The elections were marred by widespread sectarian violence, which claimed the lives of 800 people across the country, as Buhari's supporters attacked Christian settlements in the country's centre regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfr_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-cfr-77">[77]</a></sup> The three-day uprising was blamed in part on Buhari's inflammatory comments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfr_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-cfr-77">[77]</a></sup> In spite of assurances from Human Rights Watch, who had judged the elections as "among the fairest in Nigeria's history", Buhari claimed that the poll was flawed and warned<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cfr_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-cfr-77">[77]</a></sup> that "If what happened in 2011 should again happen in 2015, by the grace of God, the dog and the baboon would all be soaked in blood".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, he remains a "folk hero" to some for his vocal opposition to corruption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Buhari won 12,214,853 votes, coming in second to the incumbent president <a href="/wiki/Goodluck_Jonathan" title="Goodluck Jonathan">Goodluck Jonathan</a> of the PDP, who polled 22,495,187 votes and was declared the winner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Next20110421_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Next20110421-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2015_presidential_elections">2015 presidential elections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: 2015 presidential elections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_general_election,_2015" title="Nigerian general election, 2015">Nigerian general election, 2015</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Incoming_and_Outgoing_Nigerian_Presidents_Wave_to_Crowd_Amid_Peaceful_Inaugural_in_Abuja.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2461" data-file-width="3598" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Incoming_and_Outgoing_Nigerian_Presidents_Wave_to_Crowd_Amid_Peaceful_Inaugural_in_Abuja.jpg/220px-Incoming_and_Outgoing_Nigerian_Presidents_Wave_to_Crowd_Amid_Peaceful_Inaugural_in_Abuja.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Incoming_and_Outgoing_Nigerian_Presidents_Wave_to_Crowd_Amid_Peaceful_Inaugural_in_Abuja.jpg/330px-Incoming_and_Outgoing_Nigerian_Presidents_Wave_to_Crowd_Amid_Peaceful_Inaugural_in_Abuja.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Incoming_and_Outgoing_Nigerian_Presidents_Wave_to_Crowd_Amid_Peaceful_Inaugural_in_Abuja.jpg/440px-Incoming_and_Outgoing_Nigerian_Presidents_Wave_to_Crowd_Amid_Peaceful_Inaugural_in_Abuja.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Incoming_and_Outgoing_Nigerian_Presidents_Wave_to_Crowd_Amid_Peaceful_Inaugural_in_Abuja.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Incoming and outgoing Nigerian Presidents at the <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Muhammadu_Buhari" title="Inauguration of Muhammadu Buhari">inauguration</a> ceremony</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the runup to the 2015 Presidential elections, the campaign team of incumbent President Goodluck Jonathan asked for the disqualification of General Buhari from the election, claiming that he is in breach of the Constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> According to the fundamental document, in order to qualify for election to the office of the President, an individual must be "educated up to at least School certificate level or its equivalent". Buhari has failed to submit any such evidence, claiming that he lost the original copies of his diplomas when his house was raided following his overthrow from power in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Buhari ran in the 2015 Presidential election as a candidate of the <a href="/wiki/All_Progressives_Congress" title="All Progressives Congress">All Progressives Congress</a> party. His platform was built around his image as a staunch anti-corruption fighter and his incorruptible and honest reputation. However, Buhari stated in an interview that he would not probe past corrupt leaders and that he would give officials who stole in the past amnesty, insofar as they repent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>In January 2015, the insurgent group "The Movement for the Emancipation of the Niger Delta" (MEND) endorsed Buhari in the Presidential race, saying he is the best candidate to lead the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Muhammadu Buhari's campaign was briefly advised by former Obama campaign manager, <a href="/wiki/David_Axelrod" title="David Axelrod">David Axelrod</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> and his AKPD consultancy.</p>
<p>In February 2015, former Nigerian President Olusegun Obasanjo quit the ruling PDP party and threw his support behind the Buhari/Osinbajo ticket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 31 March, incumbent President <a href="/wiki/Goodluck_Jonathan" title="Goodluck Jonathan">Goodluck Jonathan</a> called Buhari to offer his concession and congratulations for his election as president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph-88">[88]</a></sup> Buhari was sworn in on 29 May 2015 in a <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_Muhammadu_Buhari" title="Inauguration of Muhammadu Buhari">ceremony</a> attended by at least 23 Heads of State and Government.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Abolishing_the_office_of_the_First_Lady">Abolishing the office of the First Lady</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Abolishing the office of the First Lady">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In December 2014, Muhammadu Buhari went on the record to say he would abolish, scrap or ban the office of the First Lady if he was elected as President, saying that it was unconstitutional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Movement for the Emancipation of the Niger Delta (MEND), which endorsed Buhari for the 2015 general elections, commended Buhari for his plans, when elected as President, to scrap the so-called "Office of the First Lady". MEND went on to say that the office of the first lady is obviously an irrelevant, fraudulent and unconstitutional office, whose only purpose is to further plunder the resources of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since assuming the presidency on 29 May 2015, Buhari has yet to officially abolish, scrap or ban the office of the First Lady. Aisha Buhari operates from the office of the First Lady as "wife of the President".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_religion">Freedom of religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Freedom of religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Third_GECF_summit_in_Tehran_44.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="483" data-file-width="800" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Third_GECF_summit_in_Tehran_44.jpg/220px-Third_GECF_summit_in_Tehran_44.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Third_GECF_summit_in_Tehran_44.jpg/330px-Third_GECF_summit_in_Tehran_44.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Third_GECF_summit_in_Tehran_44.jpg/440px-Third_GECF_summit_in_Tehran_44.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Third_GECF_summit_in_Tehran_44.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Buhari with Iranian President <a href="/wiki/Hassan_Rouhani" title="Hassan Rouhani">Hassan Rouhani</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Third_GECF_summit" title="Third GECF summit">Third GECF summit</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Previously, Buhari gave his support for the total implementation of <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> He was quoted in 2001 as saying, "I will continue to show openly and inside me the total commitment to the Sharia movement that is sweeping all over Nigeria", he then added: "God willing, we will not stop the agitation for the total implementation of the Sharia in the country."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 4 January 2015, while campaigning for the 2015 general election, Buhari stated that he favoured <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a>, that every Nigerian should be free and secure to practice their different religions. Buhari said, "Religion must never be used as an excuse to divide us, oppress others or gain unfair advantage. All my life I have expressed the belief that all Nigerians must worship God according to their wish".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Buhari has denied all allegations that he has a radical Islamist agenda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> On 6 January 2015, Buhari said: "Because they can't attack our record, they accuse me falsely of ethnic <a href="/wiki/Jingoism" title="Jingoism">jingoism</a>; they accuse me falsely of religious fundamentalism. Because they cannot attack our record, they accuse us falsely of calling for election violence – when we have only insisted on peace. Even as Head of State, we never imposed Sha'riah."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.282015.E2.80.93_.29">Presidency (2015– )</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Presidency (2015– )">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Buhari's first year in office, Nigeria suffered a decline in commodity prices which triggered an economic recession. To source funds to close the shortfall in revenue and fund an expansionary capital budget, Buhari travelled to 20 countries seeking loans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Thereby, expansionary budget allocation to finance infrastructure is pushed back to 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the first year of the administration, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naira" title="Naira">Naira</a>, the currency of Nigeria depreciated in the black market leading to a gulf between the official exchange rate and the black-market rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> A resulting shortage in foreign exchange hit various businesses including petroleum marketers. In May, 2016, the government announced a rise in the official pump price of petroleum to curtail shortfall in the commodity as a result of foreign exchange shortages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Niger_delta">Niger delta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Niger delta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In spite of roles played by the Ministry of Niger Delta Affairs and the Amnesty programme led by <a href="/wiki/Usani_Uguru_Usani" title="Usani Uguru Usani">Usani Uguru Usani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Boroh" title="Paul Boroh">Paul Boroh</a> respectively, to ameliorate the conditions of the people's lives and settle militant activities, they are still intermittent attacks on oil facilities by groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Niger_Delta_Avengers" title="Niger Delta Avengers">Niger Delta Avengers</a>. This has significantly affected oil production leading to cuts in exports and government revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The Avengers are waging conflict for greater economic and political autonomy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plagiarism_scandal">Plagiarism scandal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Plagiarism scandal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In September 2016 President Buhari came under heavy criticism after a newspaper report found him using plagiarized speech during launching of a national re-orientation campaign tagged “Change begins with me”. The speech was later found to be lifted from the 2008 inaugural speech of former US President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The presidency later apologized and says the blunder was caused by "overzealous staff" and "Those responsible" will be sanctioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>However, week later a deputy director in the State House linked to the speech was redeployed and presidency assured Nigerian public that it has taken steps to avoid a repeat of such an embarrassing occurrence by providing digital tools in the State House that detect plagiarism, similar to those used by editors and academics around the world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Security_challenges">Security challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Security challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2012, Buhari's name was included on a list published by Boko Haram of individuals it would trust to mediate between the group and the Federal Government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> However, Buhari strongly objected and declined to mediate between the government and Boko Haram. In 2013, Muhammadu Buhari made a series of statements, when he asked the Federal Government to stop the killing of Boko Haram members and blamed the rise of the terrorist group on the prevalence of <a href="/wiki/Conflict_in_the_Niger_Delta" title="Conflict in the Niger Delta">Niger Delta militants</a> in the South. Buhari stated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbk_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbk-109">[109]</a></sup> that "what is responsible for the security situation in the country is caused by the activities of Niger Delta militants [...] The Niger Delta militants started it all".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> He also questioned the special treatment including close to $500 million a year paid to 30,000 militants under the amnesty programme since 2013<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> by the Federal Government and deplored the fact that Boko Haram members were killed and their houses destroyed.</p>
<p>In May 2014, in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Chibok_schoolgirls_kidnapping" title="Chibok schoolgirls kidnapping">Chibok schoolgirls kidnapping</a>, Buhari strongly denounced the <a href="/wiki/Boko_Haram" title="Boko Haram">Boko Haram</a> insurgency. He "urged Nigerians to put aside religion, politics and all other divisions to crush the insurgency he said is fanned by mindless bigots masquerading as Muslims".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2014, Buhari escaped a bomb attack on his life by Boko Haram in Kaduna, 82 people were killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2014, Buhari pledged to enhance security in Nigeria, if he wins the general elections on 14 February 2015, which were later rescheduled for 28 March 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Since this announcement, Buhari's approval ratings reportedly have skyrocketed amongst the Nigerian people (largely due to the incumbent Goodluck Jonathan's apparent inability to fight Boko Haram's brutal insurgency). Buhari has now made internal security and wiping out the militant group one of the key pillars of his campaigning.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_issues">Health issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Health issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In May 2016, Buhari cancelled a two-day visit to Lagos to inaugurate projects in the state but he was represented by the Vice-President <a href="/wiki/Yemi_Osinbajo" title="Yemi Osinbajo">Yemi Osinbajo</a> after citing an "ear infection" suspected to be <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9ni%C3%A8re%27s_disease" title="Ménière's disease">Ménière's disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> On 6 June, Buhari travelled to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> to seek medical attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> This happened days after the Presidential Spokesman <a href="/wiki/Femi_Adesina" title="Femi Adesina">Femi Adesina</a> was quoted as saying Buhari was "as fit as fiddle" and "hale and hearty", to much discontent and criticism from political analysts and followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1971, Buhari married his first wife, Safinatu (née Yusuf) Buhari (First lady of Nigeria December 1983 – August 1985). They had five children together, four girls and one boy. Their first daughter, Zulaihat (Zulai) was named after Buhari's mother. Their other children are Fatima, Musa (deceased son), Hadiza, and Safinatu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naijarchives.com_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-naijarchives.com-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1988, Buhari and his first wife Safinatu divorced. In December 1989, Buhari married his second and current wife <a href="/wiki/Aisha_Buhari" title="Aisha Buhari">Aisha Buhari</a> (née Halilu). They also had five children together, a boy and four girls: Aisha, Halima, Yusuf, Zarah and Amina.</p>
<p>On 14 January 2006, Safinatu Buhari, the former first lady, died from complications of diabetes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naijarchives.com_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-naijarchives.com-121">[121]</a></sup> She was buried at Unguwar Rimi cemetery in accordance with Islamic rites.</p>
<p>In November 2012, Buhari's first daughter, Zulaihat (née Buhari) Junaid died from sickle cell anaemia, two days after having a baby at a hospital in Kaduna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards">Awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Muhammadu_Buhari&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Major-General Buhari (Rtd.) has received several awards and medals. In alphabetical order they include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Congo Medal (CM)</li>
<li>Defence Service Medal (DSM)</li>
<li>General Service Medal (GSM)</li>
<li>Global Seal of Integrity (GSOI)</li>
<li>Gran Collar De La Orden De La Independencia translated as Grand Collar of the Order of the Independence was conferred on Buhari by President Teodoro Obiang Nguema Mbasogo of Equatorial Guinea at the Presidential Palace on 14 March 2016</li>
<li>Grand Commander of the Federal Republic of Nigeria (GCFR)</li>
<li>Loyal Service and Good Conduct Medal (LSGCM)</li>
<li>National Service Medal (NSM)</li>
</ul>
